Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
8 min
Overview of the Chapter on Rahn (Pledge)
Listing of topics covered in the chapter on rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the major topics and legal issues covered in the Hanafi chapter on rahn and identify the key parties and their roles.

Core Idea
The chapter on rahn systematically addresses the obligations and rights of pledgor, mortgagee, and trustee from production to sale and maintenance.

This passage is a detailed table of contents for the chapter on rahn in the Ashraf al-Hidayah commentary. It lists dozens of specific issues the chapter covers, from the proofs of the imams to the finer points of custody, benefit, and sale of the pledged property. By surveying these topics, you will grasp the structure of the Hanafi treatment of rahn and prepare yourself to study the detailed rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Scan the topics to identify the three main parties: the pledgor (rahin), mortgagee (murtahin), and trustee (adil). Understand their basic roles from the key terms provided.
The trustee (adil) is used when neither party is trusted to hold the pledge.
2
Group the topics by party obligations
Categorize each listed topic under the appropriate party. For example, 'production of pledge' relates to the rahin, 'maintenance cost' relates to the murtahin, and 'custody by trustee' relates to the adil.
Some topics may involve multiple parties, such as 'if the mortgagee sells with permission'.
3
Identify key disputes
Note the references to Imam Zufar's argument and the Hanafis' response. The passage also mentions the second evidence of the Hanafis and the views of later scholars on the impermissibility of pawning shared property (rahn musha').
Rahn musha' is a major debated issue within the Hanafi school.
4
Recognize practical scenarios
Observe the practical questions listed: killing of a pledged slave, treatment of wounds, land tax (kharaj), tithe (ushr), etc. These indicate the chapter discusses real-life applications.
Many topics involve damage, expenses, and benefits arising during the pledge period.
5
Summarize the scope
Conclude that the chapter covers formation, possession, use, liability, sale, and termination of the pledge. It also addresses disputes and evidentiary proofs.
This overview will guide your study of the detailed rulings in subsequent lessons.

اقوال فقہاء
Ashraf al-Hidayah: Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 14. The evidence of Imam Zufar. The evidence of the Hanafis. The answer to Imam Zufar's argument. The right of the mortgagee is not fulfilled by the pledge; rather the mortgagee's property remains protected. If the mortgagee demands the debt, the pledge must be produced. If the mortgagee produced the pledge, the pledgor will first be ordered to repay the debt. If the mortgagee demands the debt from the pledgor in a city other than where the contract was made, what is the ruling? If the pledgor tells the trustee to sell the mortgaged property, then the pledgor's order is executed due to being absolute and applies to both. If the pledgor gives permission to the mortgagee to sell the mortgaged property, what is the ruling? If someone kills the slave of the pledge, what is the ruling? If the pledgor placed the mortgaged property with a trustee and gave him permission to keep it as a trust with someone else, what is the ruling? If the trustee, having placed it as a trust with his own dependents, disappears, what is the ruling? If the one with whom the trustee placed the trust denies the pledge, what is the ruling? How long can the mortgagee keep the pledge? Explanation of three issues. Mention of three issues. Can the mortgagee derive benefit from the pledge? Ruling on the mortgagee selling the pledge. From which people can the mortgagee protect the pledge? Ruling on the mortgagee's transgression regarding the pledge. Ruling on the mortgagee's use of the pledge. Ruling on hanging a mortgaged sword around the neck. Ruling on the mortgagee wearing a mortgaged ring. On whom is the expense of protecting a mortgaged oven? What things fall under the category of maintenance and preservation? Tasks that are for protection are the responsibility of the mortgagee. The viewpoint of Imam Abu Yusuf. The expense of retrieving a fugitive slave that is mortgaged is on the mortgagee. If the value of the pledge exceeds the debt, then the mortgagee is responsible for the amount of the guaranteed (portion) and the pledgor is responsible for the excess of the guaranteed. Answer to one objection. Treatment of wounds, boils, diseases, and blood money for injuries are divided between guaranteed and trust. On whom is the land tax? Is the tithe on the pledgor or mortgagee? If the pledgor pays something on behalf of the mortgagee or the mortgagee on behalf of the pledgor, it is voluntary. Statements of the jurists. Chapter on what is permissible to pawn and what is not. Ruling on pawning shared property, statements of the jurists. Second evidence of the Hanafis. Pawning shared property is absolutely impermissible. Two evidences of the late scholars for its impermissibility. Ruling on subsequent co-ownership. First evidence. Statements of the jurists.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents or list of topics covered in the chapter on rahn (pledge/pawn) from the commentary 'Ashraf al-Hidayah' (an Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah). It systematically outlines the key issues discussed: the proofs of various imams (Imam Zufar and the Hanafis), the obligations of the pledgor and mortgagee regarding production of the pledge, sale of the mortgaged property, custody and trust issues, benefit derived from the pledge (including the well-known prohibition of usufruct), transgression (ta'addi) by the mortgagee, expenses of maintenance, treatment of incidental profits, and the highly debated issue of pawning shared property (rahn musha'). The passage also mentions the second evidence of the Hanafis and the views of later scholars regarding the impermissibility of pawning undivided shares. This serves as an overview for the student to grasp the scope of the chapter before delving into detailed rulings.

Key Terms
رہن (rahn): A contract of pledge or pawn: a property left as security for a debt, which the creditor holds until the debt is repaid.
مرتہن (murtahin): The creditor/mortgagee who holds the pledge as security for a debt.
راہن (rahin): The debtor/pledgor who gives the pledge as security.
عادل (adil): A trustworthy person appointed to hold the pledge when neither party should hold it, e.g., due to lack of trust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following topics is NOT explicitly listed in the passage from the chapter on rahn?
A) Ruling on the mortgagee wearing a mortgaged ring
B) Ruling on the pledgor selling the pledged property without permission
C) Ruling on retrieving a fugitive slave that is mortgaged
D) Ruling on pawning a share of jointly owned property

Key Takeaway
The chapter on rahn covers a wide array of issues from formation to liability, with a major dispute on pawning shared property.

Navigating the Chapter on Rahn (Pledge) in Hanafi Fiqh
Index of topics covered in the Rahn chapter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major topics covered in the Hanafi chapter on rahn and explain the significance of key issues such as the pledgee's expenses and the permissibility of pledging undivided shares.

Core Idea
The chapter on rahn systematically addresses the rights and duties of pledgor and pledgee, with special attention to liability for damages and expenses.

This lesson provides an overview of the structure of the chapter on rahn (mortgage/pledge) from Ashraf al-Hidayah. Rather than diving into detailed rulings, we will map the terrain: from the definition of rahn, to the conditions of valid pledge, to special cases like salam and currency exchange. Understanding this roadmap will help you later when you study each individual ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main contract: rahn
The chapter opens with specific rulings on pledged items (sword, ring, gristmill) to illustrate the general principles. Notice that these examples highlight the pledgee's responsibilities.
The term رہن refers to the contract itself, not just the pledged property.
2
Understand the chapter's subheadings
The list moves from practical cases (treatment, maintenance, kharaj) to theoretical chapters like 'What may be pledged' and 'Ruling on musha'. This structure shows the progression from specific to general.
The chapter headings in the original text are in Arabic; the Urdu commentary adds explanations.
3
Note the key debates: musha' (undivided share)
The table highlights the Hanafi position on pledging an undivided share, including two proofs from the earlier scholars (mashayikh). This indicates a significant jurisprudential discussion.
Musha' refers to a share in a jointly owned property that is not physically divided.
4
Recognize the contract-specific sections: salam, currency exchange, rescission
The end of the list covers special contracts where rahn interacts with salam, sarf, and rescission. These are advanced topics that modify basic rules.
Understanding these requires knowledge of the underlying contracts first.

اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ۱۴۔ مرہونہ تلوار کو گلے میں لٹکانے کا حکم۔ مرتہن کیلئے مرہونہ انگوٹھی پہنے کا حکم۔ مرہونہ بھٹی کی حفاظت کی اجرت کس پر ہے۔ کون کونسی چیزیں اصلاح و ابقاء کے باب سے ہیں جو کام از باب حفاظت ہے وہ مرتہن کے ذمہ ہے امام ابو یوسف کا نقطہ نظر۔ مرہونہ بھاگے ہوئے غلام کو واپس لانے کی اجرت مرتہن پر ہے۔ رہن کی قیمت قرض سے زیادہ ہو تو مرتہن کے ذمہ بقدر مضمون ہے اور راہن کے اوپر مضمون کی زیادتی بقدر ہے۔ ایک اشکال کا جواب۔ زخم کا علاج اور پھنسی پھوڑے کا علاج اور امراض کا علاج اور جنایت کا فدیہ مضمون اور امانت پر تقسیم ہوگا خراج کس پر ہے۔ عشر راہن پر ہے یا مرتہن پر۔ راہن نے مرتہن یا مرتہن نے راہن کی طرف سے کوئی چیز ادا کر دی تو یہ تطوع ہے۔ اقوال فقهاء۔ باب ما يجوز ارتهانه والارتهان به وما لا يجوز۔ رہن مشاع کا حکم، اقول فقہاء۔ احناف کی دوسری دلیل۔ مشاع کا رہن مطلقاً نا جائز ہے۔ رہی مشائخ کے عدم جواز کی دو دلیلیں۔ شیوع طاری کا حکم۔ پہلی دلیل۔ اقوال فقہاء۔ کن چیزوں کو رہن رکھنا درست نہیں۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کا نقطہ نظر۔ درخت کو زمین کیساتھ رہن رکھنے کا حکم۔ درخت رہن رکھا اور پھل کا ذکر نہیں کیا آیا پھل رہن ہوگا یا نہیں؟ زمین، گھر اور گاؤں کے رہن میں پودے اور عمارت بھی داخل ہوں گے۔ مرہون کا کوئی مستحق نکل آئے تو کیا حکم ہے۔ کون کون سی چیز میں رہن کیلئے مانفع اور رکاوٹ ہیں کن کن اشیاء کا رہن درست نہیں۔ مضمون بغیرہ کا رہن بھی درست نہیں۔ مضمون بعينها کے بدلے رہن درست ہے۔ رہن بالدرک باطل اور کفالت بالدرک جاتا ہے وجہ فرق۔ مشتری نے وجوب سے پہلے رہن پر قبضہ کر لیا پھر ہلاک ہو گیا تو ضمان نہ ہوگا۔ دین موعود کے بدلے رہن کا حکم۔ بیع سلم کے راس المال، بیع صرف کے شمن اور مسلم فیہ کے بدلے رہن کا حکم مبیع کے بدلے رہن کا حکم بیچ الصرف کے ثمن کے بدلے اور راس المال کے بدلے رہن رکھا گیا ابھی مجلس متحد تھی کہ مرہونہ شی ہلاک ہوگئی تو بیع صرف اور بیع سلم کو نام شمار کیا جائے گا مسلم فیہ کے بدلے رہن ہلاک ہو جائے تو بیع سلم باطل ہے۔ مسلم الیہ اور رب السلام نے بیع سلم کو فتح کر دیا اور مسلم فیہ کے عوض رہن تھا تو رہن رأس المال کے عوض ہو جائے گا۔ اگر تفاسخ کے بعد مرہون ہلاک ہو جائے تو سلم فیہ (غلہ) کے بدلے ہلاک ہو گیا۔ مسئلہ مذکورہ کی ایک مثال۔
Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah Volume 14. Ruling on hanging a pledged sword around the neck. Ruling on the pledgee wearing a pledged ring. Who bears the maintenance cost of a pledged gristmill? What things are from the chapter of repair and preservation; tasks that are for preservation are the responsibility of the pledgee – viewpoint of Imam Abu Yusuf. The cost of retrieving a fugitive slave is on the pledgee. If the value of the pledge exceeds the debt, the pledgee is liable to the extent of what is guaranteed and the pledgor is liable for the excess. Answer to an objection. Treatment of wounds, boils, diseases, and bloodwit for injuries are divided between what is guaranteed and what is a trust; who bears the kharaj? Is the tithe on the pledgor or the pledgee? If the pledgor or pledgee pays something on behalf of the other, it is voluntary. Sayings of jurists. Chapter: What may be pledged and what may be taken as a pledge and what not. Ruling on a musha' (undivided share) pledge, sayings of jurists. Second proof of the Hanafis. Pledge of a musha' is absolutely impermissible. Two proofs of the earlier scholars (mashayikh) for impermissibility. Ruling on a defect that occurs. First proof. Sayings of jurists. Which things are not valid to pledge. Viewpoint of Imam Abu Hanifah. Ruling on pledging a tree along with the land. If a tree is pledged and no mention is made of its fruit, is the fruit included in the pledge? In pledging land, house, or village, the plants and buildings are also included. What is the ruling if someone else claims ownership of the pledged property? In which things is there benefit and obstacle for pledge? Which items are not valid for pledge? Pledge of a guaranteed thing (madhmun bi-ghayrihi) is also not valid. Pledge of the specific thing is valid. A pledge for security (rahn bi-al-darak) is void, but a guarantee (kafalah bi-al-darak) is valid; reason for the difference. If the buyer took possession of the pledge before the obligation arises and then it perishes, there is no liability. Ruling on pledging for a future debt. Ruling on pledging the capital in salam sale, the price in currency exchange, and the subject matter in salam; ruling on pledging for the sold item. If a thing is pledged for the price in currency exchange and for the capital in salam, and while the session is still unified the pledged property perishes, the currency exchange and salam are considered void. If the pledged property perishes for the subject matter of salam, the salam is void. If the seller (muslam ilayh) and the buyer (rabb al-salam) rescind the salam contract, and the pledge was for the subject matter, then the pledge becomes for the capital. If the pledged property perishes after rescission, it perishes for the salam subject matter (grain). An example of the mentioned issue.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This is a table of contents from Ashraf al-Hidayah, an Urdu commentary on Al-Hidayah by Burhan al-Din al-Marghinani. The list covers the chapter on rahn (mortgage/pledge) from Vol. 14. It outlines topics including the pledgee's right to use the pledge, responsibilities for maintenance, the status of fruits and buildings attached to the pledged property, rulings on musha' (undivided share), and various contract-specific issues like salam, currency exchange, and rescission. The author uses this as a navigation aid for the detailed discussions that follow.

Key Terms
رہن (rahn): A contract where property is given as security for a debt
مرہون (marhun): The property that is pledged as security
مرتہن (murtahin): The creditor/pledgee who receives the pledge
راہن (rahin): The debtor/pledgor who gives the pledge

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The table of contents provides a roadmap for the Rahn chapter, highlighting key debates on liability, permissibility of musha', and special contract interactions.

Navigating the Rahn Chapter: Overview of Pledge Rulings
Table of contents for Rahn chapter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main topics covered in the Hanafi chapter on Rahn (pledge) and locate the page numbers for specific rulings.

Core Idea
The table of contents maps the logical sequence of Rahn rulings: from valid pledges to disputes among the founding imams.

This lesson orients you to Volume 14 of Ashraf al-Hidayah, which contains the complete Urdu commentary on the Rahn chapter of Al-Hidayah. The table of contents is your first tool—it reveals the structure and progression of rulings, from fundamental cases (e.g., pledging a slave) to detailed disagreements between Imam Abu Hanifa and his students. By understanding this map, you will navigate the volume with confidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the volume and chapter
Confirm that Volume 14 covers Rahn (pledge). The TOC begins with entries like 'A slave purchased with a corrupt sale' and then moves to specific pledge rulings.
The title 'Ashraf al-Hidayah' and the page header confirm this.
2
Scan the main topic groups
Group the entries: permissible pledges, impermissible items (wine, carrion), pledging a child's property by father/guardian, executor's role, and disputes on specific items like a silver pot.
Look for recurring keywords like 'حکم' (ruling), 'دلیل' (evidence), 'قول' (saying).
3
Note the page numbers
Each topic is assigned a page number. Use these to quickly locate detailed discussions.
Page numbers may have OCR errors; verify with the actual book.
4
Recognize the dispute sections
The TOC entries on 'Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence' and 'Imam Muhammad's viewpoint' indicate where intra-madhhab disagreements are explained.
These sections are crucial for understanding Hanafi methodology.
5
Preview the end of the chapter
The last entries cover rulings on guarantor and istihsan, showing the chapter's conclusion.
This helps you see the full scope before diving in.

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴
غلام کو شراء فاسد کیساتھ خریدا اور اس کا شمن ادا کر دیا مشتری کو حق ہے کہ مبیع کو روکے رکھے تاکہ ثمن وصول کرے
تر مدل بر ، مکاتب ، ام ولد کو بطور رہن رکھنے کا حکم
جن چیزوں کے بدلے رہن رکھنا جائز نہیں
مسلمان کیلئے شراب رہن رکھنا اور شراب کا رہن قبول کرنا جائز نہیں
مردار کے رہن رکھنے کا حکم
آزاد، شراب، مردار کا پہلے سے علم نہ ہو ان کے رہن کا حکم
باپ صغیر کے مال کو رہن کے طور پر رکھ سکتا ہے یا نہیں

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for Volume 14 of 'Ashraf al-Hidayah', a complete Urdu commentary on 'Al-Hidayah' by Burhan al-Din al-Marghinani. Volume 14 covers the chapter of Rahn (pledge/pawn) in Hanafi fiqh. The TOC lists topics in sequential order, beginning with rulings on purchasing a slave with a corrupt sale and the buyer's right to retain the item, then moving to the permissibility of pledging specific individuals (mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad), items that cannot be pledged (wine, carrion), and the pledging of a child's property by a father or guardian (wasi). Later entries discuss the sale of a pledged item, destruction of the pledge, and disagreements between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on cases like pledging a silver pot. The index provides a roadmap for the detailed fiqh discussions that follow in the main text.

Key Terms
رہن (rahn): The act of pledging property as security for a debt, where the pledged item remains in the possession of the creditor until the debt is repaid.
مرہون (marhun): The property that is pledged as security (the pawned item).
مرتہن (murtahin): The creditor who holds the pledged property as security.
وصی (wasi): An executor or guardian appointed to manage the affairs of a minor or orphan.

Navigating the Topics of the Rahn (Pledge) Chapter
Index of topics for the Rahn chapter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key legal issues discussed in the Hanafi chapter on Rahn and distinguish between the roles of the parties involved (raahin, murtahin, 'adl).

Core Idea
The Rahn chapter covers more than pawning: it addresses agency, multiple debts, and the custodian's liability.

This passage is a topical index for the chapter on Rahn (pledge) from volume 14 of Ashraf al-Hidayah. It lists dozens of legal questions that the chapter resolves, from the basic contract to complex scenarios involving multiple creditors, death of parties, and appointment of agents. Before diving into the detailed rulings, it is helpful to survey these topics to understand the scope of Hanafi law on pledging.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Read through the list and note the frequent mentions of raahin (pledgor), murtahin (pledgee), 'adl (trustee), and wakil (agent). Understand their legal functions: the pledgor gives possession, the pledgee holds security, the trustee acts as a neutral custodian, and the agent may sell the pawn at maturity.
The 'adl is a special feature in Hanafi law, appointed when the parties distrust each other or the pawn is difficult to value.
2
Categorize the issues
Group the listed headings into categories: formation and validity of the contract (e.g., specifying multiple pawns), possession and liability (e.g., destruction of the pawn), death of parties, agency (wakala), and sale of the pawn. This reveals the logical structure of the chapter.
The index itself does not provide rulings; it is a map for finding detailed expositions.
3
Note the intra-madhhab viewpoints
The index explicitly mentions 'Imam Zufr's viewpoint' and 'Imam Muhammad's viewpoint'. These indicate that within the Hanafi school there are divergences on certain issues. Recognising these names helps you anticipate where the commentary will present alternative positions.
Imam Zufr and Imam Muhammad are students of Abu Hanifa; their views often appear as secondary opinions.
4
Recognize the role of the 'adl
A separate sub-chapter is devoted to pawning with an 'adl. The headings discuss his liability, his obligation to hand over the pawn, and the consequences of his actions. This underscores the importance of neutral custody in Hanafi pledge law.
The 'adl is not a guarantor; he is only liable for misconduct or negligence.
5
Understand the function of this index
This passage is a memorization anchor and reference tool. Each line corresponds to a detailed discussion elsewhere in the volume. When studying or practicing, you can use this index to quickly locate specific rulings.
Do not mistake the index for the actual rulings; always refer to the main text for full legal reasoning.

If the trustworthy person sells the pawn, it leaves the state of pawn and the price becomes a substitute in pawn. If someone kills the pawned slave, due to which liability for the price is incurred, then will that liability be considered as pawn? If the trustworthy person sells the pawn at the time of debt payment according to the agency and gives the price to the pledgee, or if someone else turns out to be entitled to the price of the pawn, what is the ruling?

Commentary
This passage is a tabulation of the principal legal questions (masa'il) discussed in the chapter on Rahn (pledge) from volume 14 of Ashraf al-Hidayah, an Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah. The text serves as an index or a summary of topics, not as a detailed exposition of individual rulings. The listing covers issues such as the ruling when a purchaser refuses to deliver the mortgaged item, the effect of specifying multiple items and debts, the disposition of the pawn after destruction or payment, the role of a trustee ('adl), and the rules of appointing an agent for sale. Each line represents a distinct legal problem that is elaborated elsewhere in the volume. The presence of 'Imam Zufr's viewpoint' and 'Imam Muhammad's viewpoint' indicates intra-madhhab differences, but the passage does not provide the actual rulings or proofs. This is typical of a fihrist (table of contents) meant to aid memorization and quick reference.

Key Terms
رہن (rahn): The object pledged as security for a debt. In this text, it refers to the item placed as collateral.
راہن (raahin): The person who pledges his property as security (the pledgor).
مرتہن (murtahin): The person who receives the pledge as security for a debt (the pledgee).
عادل ('adl): A trustworthy person appointed to hold the pawned item, often when there is dispute or need for impartial custody.

Injury to the Pledged Item: Liability of Rahin and Murtahin
Explains rulings on injury by rahin, murtahin, and third party to marhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on injury by the rahin and murtahin to the marhun and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The rahin's injury is compensable; the murtahin's injury cancels debt proportionally.

When a pledged item (marhun) is damaged or injured, the liability depends on who caused the harm. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling when the pledgor (rahin) or the pledgee (murtahin) injures the marhun. The school holds that the rahin must compensate for the damage, while the murtahin's injury reduces the debt by the value of the loss. However, the two main disciples, the Sahibayn, held a different view. We will explore this ikhtilaf and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Determine who caused the injury: rahin, murtahin, or a third party. The ruling differs for each.
The marhun remains the property of the rahin, but possession is with the murtahin.
2
State the ruling for rahin's injury
If the rahin injures the marhun, he must pay compensation for the damage. The debt remains unaffected.
This is based on trust: the rahin has a duty of care.
3
State the ruling for murtahin's injury
If the murtahin injures the marhun, the debt is reduced by the amount of the injury. The murtahin does not pay separate compensation.
This cancels debt proportionate to the injury value.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds the above. The Sahibayn argue that the murtahin's injury does not cancel debt but requires cash payment. Understand their proofs (not fully quoted in the text).
The dispute affects whether the debt is reduced or the murtahin pays cash.
5
Apply the ruling to other scenarios
Consider cases where the marhun's value fluctuates, or a third party kills the slave. Apply the same principles.
Price changes do not cancel debt; the marhun remains a pledge for the full debt.

Source Text

How many types of marhun [؟] are there? The rahin's injury on the marhun is guaranteed. The murtahin's injury on the marhun cancels the debt to the extent of the injury. The ruling on injury by rahin, murtahin, or both on the marhun or their property: statements of the jurists. The proof of the Sahibayn. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa. Ruling: if the marhun injures the rahin or murtahin and the value of the marhun is more than the debt. Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings on jinaayat (injuries/torts) involving the marhun (pledged item). It opens with the question of how many types of marhun exist, but the OCR cuts off the answer. It then states that if the rahin (pledgor) injures the marhun, he is liable for the damage (madhmun). If the murtahin (pledgee) injures the marhun, the debt is reduced proportional to the injury. The passage then introduces a dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the ruling when the rahin or murtahin injures the marhun or their own property. Following this, it addresses a scenario where the marhun (a slave) injures its owner or the creditor: if the slave's value exceeds the debt, special rules apply. A further case involves a slave pledged at a value equal to the debt, whose price drops and then rises again; the Hanafi position is that the debt is not cancelled by price fluctuation, so the marhun remains liable for the full debt. The passage also covers the sale of the marhun by the murtahin upon the rahin's order, where proceeds partially clear the debt and the remainder stays with the rahin. Finally, it asks about a third party killing the pledged slave without legal liability (inadvertently), a case that requires further examination. The commentary is a summary of rulings from the Hidayah, with references to the proofs of the imams, though the actual evidence is not quoted verbatim.

Key Terms
مرہون (marhun): The item pledged as security for a debt; in this text, it can be a slave or other property.
جنایت (jinaayah): Injury or tort; here it means any harmful act causing damage to the marhun or its value.
صاحبین (al-Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Milking a Mortgaged Goat with Permission
Ruling on pledgee's use of milk with permission and debt adjustment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a pledgee's use of a mortgaged animal with permission and explain the effect on debt if the animal dies.

Core Idea
Permission removes liability for use; collateral value remains secured.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in mortgage (rahn) law: what happens when the pledgee (murtahin) uses the pledged property—here a goat's milk—with the pledgor's (rahin) permission. The Hanafi school distinguishes between authorized and unauthorized use, and this ruling clarifies the financial consequences for the debt if the collateral perishes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Determine who the pledgor (rahin) and pledgee (murtahin) are, and the act of milking and consuming the mortgaged goat's milk with the pledgor's explicit permission.
Permission is key — without it, the ruling would differ.
2
Determine liability for use
With permission, the pledgee incurs no guarantee (zaman) for consuming the milk. The permission authorizes the use, so no compensation is due.
This is the primary ruling: permission negates liability.
3
Analyze the goat's death scenario
If the goat dies while still in pledge and not redeemed, the debt is divided between the goat's value and the value of the milk consumed. The portion of the debt equal to the goat's value is reduced (i.e., forgiven), but the milk consumed does not reduce the debt further.
The milk is considered a separate benefit that does not offset the debt.
4
Understand the legal rationale
Permission allows use without liability, but the goat remains collateral securing the debt. Upon its death, its value extinguishes part of the debt proportionally, while the benefit (milk) is already consumed with permission.
The debt is not fully forgiven; only the goat's value portion is reduced.
5
Consider cross-madhhab differences
Other madhhabs may hold that any use of collateral, even with permission, creates liability because the collateral should remain untouched. The Hanafi position is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and the permission of the owner.
This ikhtilaf affects the practical outcome in cases of loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر راہن مرتہن کو مرہونہ بکری سے دودھ دوہنے اور پینے کی اجازت دے دے تو مرتہن پر منمان نہیں ہوگا راہن نے بکری نہیں چھڑائی کہ مرتہن کے پاس ہلاک ہوگئی تو قرض کو بکری اور جو دودھ پیا اس پر تقسیم کیا جائے گا تو قرض کی جو مقدار بکری کے بدلے آئے گی وہ ساقط ہو جائے گی
If the pledgor gives permission to the pledgee to milk and drink from the mortgaged goat, then there is no guarantee (liability) on the pledgee. If the pledgor did not redeem the goat and it died with the pledgee, then the debt will be divided between the goat and the milk drunk. Whatever amount of the debt is matched by the goat will be reduced.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the use of a mortgaged animal. If the pledgor (rahin) explicitly permits the pledgee (murtahin) to milk and consume the milk of a mortgaged goat, the pledgee is not held liable for consuming the milk. However, if the goat dies while still in the pledgee's possession, the value of the goat and the consumed milk are both deducted from the debt proportionally. This is because permission authorizes use, and the goat is collateral for the debt.

Key Terms
راہن (rahin): The person who pledges property as security for a debt (pledgor).
مرتہن (murtahin): The person who receives the pledged property as security (pledgee).
مرہون (marhun): The property that is pledged as collateral.
منمان (zaman): Liability or guarantee; the obligation to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The full debt remains, and the pledgee must pay for the goat.
B) The debt is reduced by the value of the goat only; the milk consumed does not affect the debt.
C) The debt is completely forgiven.
D) The debt is reduced by the combined value of the goat and the milk consumed.

Key Takeaway
Permission to use collateral removes liability for the benefit consumed, but the collateral's value remains part of the debt security.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Increase in Mortgage (Rahn)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mortgage increase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Yusuf and al-Tarafayn regarding increase in mortgage, and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Unintentional increase in mortgage is valued at redemption, not as part of the original pledge.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement on how increases (ziyadah) in mortgaged property affect the contract. Imam Abu Yusuf held that increases become part of the mortgage, while the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) considered them separate trusts valued at redemption. Understanding this dispute clarifies the school's methodology in balancing qiyas and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the increase in mortgage? Offspring, fruit, or other natural growth. Does it become part of the pledge or remain separate?
The term 'ziyadah' refers to any increment connected to the mortgaged property.
2
State Imam Abu Yusuf's position
Imam Abu Yusuf likely considered any increase as part of the mortgage, based on the pledgee's possession (yad).
The exact proof is not preserved, but it may rely on qiyas.
3
State the Tarafayn's position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad held that unintentional increase (e.g., offspring) is a separate trust (amanah) and is valued at yawm al-fikak.
They objected to Abu Yusuf's view, likely on istihsan grounds.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Work through a concrete scenario: a mortgaged cow gives birth. Under Abu Yusuf, the calf is part of the mortgage; under the Tarafayn, it is separate and its value reduces the debt.
This practical difference shows the impact of each position.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The final statement indicates the relied-upon view: unintentional increase is valued at yawm fikak, i.e., the Tarafayn's position.
This is the operating ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رہن اور دین میں زیادتی کا حکم امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل طرفین کی دلیل طرفین کی طرف سے امام ابو یوسف پر اعتراض رہن میں زیادتی کا حکم غیر ارادی زیادتی اس کے مقصود ہونے کی وجہ سے یوم فکاک کی قیمت معتبر ہوتی ہے
The ruling on increase in mortgage and debt. The evidence of Imam Abu Yusuf. The evidence of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Objection to Imam Abu Yusuf from the two. The ruling on increase in mortgage. Unintentional increase, because it is intended, the value on the day of redemption is considered.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage begins a discussion on how increases (ziyadah) in the mortgaged property or the debt affect the contract. It first states the ruling, then presents the evidence of Imam Abu Yusuf, followed by the evidence of the other two (al-Tarafayn: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani), and then an objection to Imam Abu Yusuf from the Tarafayn. The final statement indicates that for unintentional increase that is intended (i.e., natural growth like offspring or fruit), the valuation is made on the day of redemption (yawm al-fikak). This point of contention: Imam Abu Yusuf likely considered any increase as part of the mortgage unless explicitly excluded, while the Tarafayn held that certain increases are separate.

Key Terms
زیادتی (ziyadah): Increase or addition to the mortgaged property or debt, such as offspring, fruit, or profit.
یوم فکاک (yawm al-fikak): The day of redemption; when the pledgor repays the debt and reclaims the pledged property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) At the time of redemption (yawm al-fikak)
C) At the time the increase occurs
D) At the time the debt is incurred

Key Takeaway
Unintentional increase in a mortgage is a separate trust valued at redemption, not an automatic part of the pledge.

When a Mortgaged Slave is Replaced: Hanafi Ruling
States ruling when pledgor replaces mortgaged property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a mortgaged property is replaced with another of equal value, and explain why the original remains mortgaged.

Core Idea
Substitution does not cancel a mortgage; the original remains mortgaged until returned.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on a specific scenario in mortgage (rahn): when the pledgor (rahin) replaces the mortgaged property with another item of equal value. The ruling clarifies that the original mortgage contract remains intact until the pledgee (murtahin) physically returns the original property. The replacement is held as a trust (amanah), not as a mortgage. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of rahn in property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Parties
Recognize that the pledgor (rahin) has placed a slave as mortgage, then substitutes it with another slave of equal value. The pledgee (murtahin) holds both slaves initially.
The key question: does substitution cancel the original mortgage?
2
Determine the Ruling for the Original Property
According to the Hanafi school, the original slave remains in the state of mortgage (rahn) as long as it has not been physically returned to the pledgor. The mortgage is not automatically transferred to the new slave.
The condition for the original to remain mortgaged is that it is still held by the pledgee.
3
Determine the Status of the Replacement Property
The new slave is held by the pledgee as a trust (amanah), not as a mortgage. This means the pledgee is not liable for loss or damage unless due to negligence.
The replacement does not become part of the mortgage contract; it is a separate amanah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
راہن نے ایسا غلام رکھا جس کی قیمت ہزار ہے پھر اس کی جگہ دوسرا غلام اتنی ہی قیمت کا رکھا تو مرتہن جب تک پہلے کو لوٹا نہ دے پہلا ر ہن ہے اور دوسرا غلام امانت ہے
If the pledgor kept a slave whose value is one thousand, then placed another slave of the same value in its place, then the pledgee, as long as he does not return the first, the first is a mortgage and the second slave is a trust.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This ruling addresses the situation where the pledgor (rahin) substitutes one mortgaged property for another. According to the Hanafi school, if the pledgor replaces a slave worth 1000 with another slave of equal value, the original slave remains in the state of mortgage (rahn) until the pledgee (murtahin) physically returns it to the pledgor. The new slave is held by the pledgee as a trust (amanah), not as a mortgage. This is because the mortgage contract is specific to the original property; substitution without release of the original does not transfer the mortgage to the new property.

Key Terms
امانت (amanah): A trust; property held without liability for loss unless due to negligence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is no longer in mortgage
C) It remains a mortgage until returned
D) It becomes the property of the pledgee

Key Takeaway
When a pledgor substitutes mortgaged property with another of equal value, the original remains mortgaged until physically returned; the substitute is held as a trust.

Ibra vs Ibqa: Liability for Pledged Property After Release
Defines the difference between ibra and ibqa in rahn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ibra and ibqa in the context of rahn and state the ruling on the pledgee's liability when the mortgaged property perishes after release or retention.

Core Idea
Ibra ends liability; ibqa preserves it — the difference turns on debt discharge.

When a mortgaged property perishes while in the pledgee's possession, the ruling depends on whether the debt has been released (ibra) or retained (ibqa). This passage explains that after ibra, the pledgee is not liable, while after ibqa, he may be liable. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct application in transactions involving collateral.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Read the passage and identify the terms ibra (ابراء) and ibqa (ابقاء). Note that ibra means release or absolution of the debt, while ibqa means retention of the mortgage.
2
State the ruling for each
The passage explicitly says: 'In ibra, there is no guarantee from the mortgaged property perishing with the pledgee.' For ibqa, it implies that in the mentioned case guarantee is wajib. Contrast the two rulings.
The 'mentioned case' likely refers to ibqa where the property perishes without release.
3
Understand the 'illa (reason for difference)
The passage ends with 'the reason for the difference between ibra and ibqa'. Consider that in ibra, the debt is discharged, so the pledgee no longer has a right to hold the property; its loss is on the owner. In ibqa, the debt remains, so the pledgee retains responsibility.
This is a talil (reasoning) passage.
4
Clarify the reference to four cases
The passage mentions 'four cases' and 'woman'. Although text is ambiguous, it likely refers to scenarios where liability is waived under certain conditions. For the main lesson, focus on the core distinction.
OCR risk: high; the exact four cases are not essential for this lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابراء میں مرہون مرتہن کے پاس ہلاک ہونے سے ضمان نہیں ہے مذکورہ چار صورتوں میں عورت پر منمان نہیں مذکورہ صورت میں ضمان واجب ہے ابراء اور ابقاء کے درمیان وجہ فرق
In ibra (release), there is no guarantee from the mortgaged property perishing with the pledgee. In the mentioned four cases, there is no liability on the woman. In the mentioned case, guarantee is wajib. The reason for the difference between ibra and ibqa.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage discusses two concepts: ibra (release/absolution of the debt) and ibqa (retention of the mortgage). The key distinction is that if the mortgagee (pledgee) releases the debt (ibra) while the mortgaged property is still with him, and the property later perishes, he is not liable for its value. In contrast, if the mortgage is retained (ibqa), the pledgee may be liable. The reference to four cases involving a woman is unclear from this text but likely pertains to specific scenarios where liability is waived or imposed. The final statement indicates that the underlying reason ('illa) for the difference is explained.

Key Terms
ابراء (ibra): Release or absolution of the debt by the creditor, freeing the debtor from obligation.
ابقاء (ibqa): Retention of the mortgage; keeping the pledged property as security for the debt without releasing it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The debt is revived.
C) There is no guarantee on the pledgee.
D) The pledgee is partially liable.

Key Takeaway
Ibra releases the pledgee from liability if the collateral perishes, because the debt no longer exists as a basis for holding the property.

Ruling on Hawala When Collateral Perishes After Payment
Presents a case of hawala where the collateral perishes after debt payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when collateral perishes after the transferee pays the debt in a hawala contract, and explain the transferee's right to recovery.

Core Idea
In hawala, if collateral perishes after payment, the debt is extinguished and the transferee reclaims from the transferor.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a nuanced case combining hawala (transfer of debt) and rahn (pledge). Understanding the interplay between these contracts is crucial for correctly allocating liability when the collateral is lost after the debt is satisfied. The ruling clarifies that the transferor bears the risk in this scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and sequence
First, understand the chain: a debtor (transferor) transfers his debt to a transferee (muhal alayh) via hawala. The transferor had pledged collateral (rahn) with a mortgagee (murtahin). The transferee pays the debt. Before the mortgagee returns the collateral, it perishes.
Hawala transfers the debt but not the collateral.
2
Determine the effect on the original debt
The ruling states: the debt is extinguished (saqit). Since the transferee paid, the original debtor's obligation is discharged.
3
Determine the transferee's right to recover
The transferee (muhal alayh) can reclaim the amount he paid from the transferor (original debtor) because the collateral's loss is effectively the transferor's responsibility.
The text says: 'muhal alayh ka wapas karna zaruri hoga'.
4
Understand the second scenario: mutual agreement of no debt
If both pledgor and mortgagee agree that there is no debt, then if the collateral perishes with the mortgagee, it is considered 'mazmun bil dain' (covered by the debt) – meaning the mortgagee is not liable for its value.
This scenario differs: the debt is already cancelled, so the collateral's loss is treated as part of the original debt arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص اپنے دین کا حوالہ کر دیا اور محتال علیہ نے قرض ادا کر دیا ابھی مرتہن نے مرہون کو واپس نہیں کیا تھا کہ وہ ہلاک ہو گیا تو قرض ساقط ہوگا اور محتال علیہ کا واپس کرنا ضروری ہوگا را ہن اور مرہون دونوں اتفاق کر لیں کہ قرض نہیں ہے اب مرہون مرتہن کے پاس ہلاک ہو جائے تو مضمون بالدین ہوگا
A person transferred his debt (hawala), and the transferee (muhal 'alayh) paid the debt. The mortgagee had not yet returned the mortgaged property when it perished. Then the debt will be extinguished, and the transferee must get his money back. If the pledgor and mortgagee both agree that there is no debt, then when the collateral perishes with the pledgee, it will be considered as part of the debt (mazmun bil dain).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a complex case involving hawala (transfer of debt) and rahn. A person transfers his debt to another (muhal 'alayh), who pays it. Before the mortgagee returns the collateral, it perishes. The ruling: the original debt is extinguished (saqit), and the transferee can recover his payment from the transferor because the collateral's death is essentially a loss of the security. The final sentence adds: if both the pledgor (rahin) and mortgagee (murtahin) agree that there is no debt (i.e., the debt was cancelled), then if the collateral perishes with the mortgagee, it is considered 'mazmun bil dain' (covered by the debt), meaning the mortgagee is not liable for its value.

Key Terms
حوالہ (hawala): Transfer of debt from one person to another, with the transferee obligated to pay.
محتال علیہ (muhal alayh): The person to whom the debt is transferred; the transferee who becomes liable to pay.
مضمون بالدین (mazmun bil dain): Covered by the debt; i.e., the collateral's value is considered part of the debt obligation.
ساقط (saqit): Extinguished; the debt is considered fully discharged.
مرتہن (murtahin): Mortgagee; the person holding the collateral as security.
راہن (rahin): Pledgor; the person who gives collateral.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hawala case, if the mortgagee had not returned the collateral and it perishes after the transferee pays the debt, what is the ruling?
A) The transferee loses his money, and the original debt remains.
B) The debt is extinguished, and the transferee must recover his payment from the transferor.
C) The mortgagee must pay the value of the collateral to the transferee.
D) The original debt is revived.

Key Takeaway
In a hawala with collateral, if the transferee pays the debt and the collateral perishes before return, the debt is extinguished and the transferee can reclaim from the transferor.

Definition and Legitimacy of Rahn (Pledge)
Defines rahn and proves its permissibility with four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal definition of rahn and identify the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) that establish its permissibility in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Rahn (pledge) is a permissible contract of security, proven by Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy to kafalah.

This lesson introduces the contract of rahn (pledge/pawn), a key security transaction in Islamic finance. We will first clarify its linguistic and legal definitions, then examine the four classical proofs that establish its permissibility. Understanding how the Hanafi school derives the ruling from these sources is essential for grasping the structure of deferred payment security.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic vs legal meaning
Identify the two definitions: linguistically 'detaining something', legally 'detaining property as security for a debt repayable from it'.
The legal definition adds the purpose of securing a right.
2
Identify the four proofs cited
List the four sources: Quran (2:283), Sunnah (hadith of the Prophet's armor), Ijma (consensus), and Qiyas (analogy to kafalah).
These are the primary sources of Islamic law.
3
Examine the Quranic verse and its scope
Analyze the verse: it mentions rahn in the context of travel. Understand that it is a permission, not a restriction. The Sunnah shows it is valid in residence too.
The verse is not a condition but an example.
4
Understand the analogy to kafalah
See how rahn is analogized to guarantee (kafalah) based on the common element of strengthening the fulfillment of a right.
Qiyas requires a shared illa (effective cause).
5
Note the consensus
Recognize that scholars have unanimously agreed on the permissibility of rahn, making it a settled ruling.
Ijma adds certainty to the ruling.

Kitab al-Rahn
Translation: This book is about the issues of rahn.
Kitab al-Rahn
Explanation: The appropriateness between Kitab al-Sayd and Kitab al-Rahn is that both aim at acquiring wealth. Then rahn is a verbal noun meaning to pawn, and sometimes a verbal noun is used in the sense of a passive participle, so rahn sometimes means marhun (the pledged item). Rahin is the one who pledges his property with another, and murtahin is the one with whom it is pledged.
Linguistic and legal meaning of rahn:
Al-rahn linguistically is the detention of a thing by any cause. In Shari'ah, it is making a thing detained for a right that can be fulfilled from the rahn, such as debts. It is legislated by the saying of Allah Most High: 'farihanun maqbuda' (then a pledged bond), and by what is narrated that the Prophet (SAW) bought grain from a Jew and pawned his coat of mail with him. Consensus has been reached on this. And because it is a contract of security for the side of fulfillment, so it is analogized to security on the side of obligation, which is kafalah.
Translation: Rahn linguistically means to detain something for any reason, and in Shari'ah it means to detain something in exchange for a right whose fulfillment from the rahn is possible, like a debt. And rahn is legislated by the command of Allah: 'farihanun maqbuda', and because it is narrated that the Prophet (SAW) bought grain from a Jew and pawned his coat of mail with him. Consensus has been established on its permissibility. And because rahn is a contract that strengthens the side of fulfillment, it is analogized to the strengthening of the side of obligation, which is kafalah.
Explanation: Firstly, the author mentioned the linguistic and legal meanings of rahn. He says that rahn means to detain something for any reason, and in Shari'ah rahn is to detain something in exchange for a debt so that at the time of need the debt can be fulfilled from the rahn. Then the author said that rahn is a permissible contract. Its permissibility is proven from the Book of Allah, the Sunnah, consensus, and analogy.
Book of Allah: 'And if you are on a journey and cannot find a scribe, then a pledge with possession' meaning if you make a transaction of loan or credit in travel and cannot find a scribe for the document, then some property should be given as a pledge to the debtor. The need for rahn in travel is greater than in residence because in residence satisfaction of the creditor is possible easily through writing and witnesses. Therefore, the command for rahn is in travel, not in residence. However, rahn is also valid in the presence of a scribe, as mentioned in the hadith.
Summary: The permissibility of rahn is proven from this verse of the Quran.
Sunnah of the Messenger: Bukhari and Muslim narrate that the Prophet (SAW) bought grain from a Jew and pawned his coat of mail to him as payment.
Thus, from the hadith it is known that rahn is permissible.
Consensus: There is consensus on the permissibility of rahn.
Analogy: Kafalah is unanimously permissible, and its reality is that an obligatory right is received from the guarantor, so strengthening of the side of fulfillment occurs due to kafalah. Similarly, due to rahn, the side of fulfilling the right is strengthened. So rahn is analogized to kafalah, and it is said: Just as kafalah is permissible, similarly rahn is permissible.
Summary: The permissibility of the contract of rahn is proven from the four proofs.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage introduces the book of rahn (pledge/pawn). The author first explains the linguistic and legal definitions of rahn. Linguistically, rahn means to detain something; legally, it means to detain a property as security for a debt that can be fulfilled from it. The permissibility of rahn is established through four sources: the Quran (Surah al-Baqarah, verse 283: 'farihanun maqbuda'), the Sunnah (the Prophet's pawning his armor to a Jew), consensus (ijma) of the scholars, and analogy (qiyas) to kafalah (guarantee). The analogy is based on the common element of securing a right. Rahn is a contract of security to strengthen the likelihood of debt repayment. The passage also clarifies that although the Quranic verse mentions rahn in the context of travel, the Sunnah shows it is also valid in residence, as the Prophet performed it in Madinah while at home. Thus, rahn is a legitimate contract (mubah) by all four primary proofs.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): Linguistically: detaining something. Legally: making a property detained as security for a debt.
راہن (rahin): The person who pledges his property.
مرتہن (murtahin): The person to whom the property is pledged (creditor).
مرہون (marhun): The pledged property itself.
عقد تبرع (aqd tabarru'): A gratuitous contract where benefit is given without exchange.
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking hold of the pledged property.
لزوم (luzum): Bindingness; the contract becomes irrevocable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Rahn is a permissible contract of security supported by the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy to kafalah, and is not restricted to travel.

Constitution of Rahn: Ijab, Qabul, and the Role of Possession
Dispute on the necessity of possession for rahn's bindingness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires possession (qabd) for the bindingness of rahn and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
Rahn is valid upon offer and acceptance, but becomes binding only with possession; Malik differs.

This lesson explores the formation and bindingness of the pledge (rahn) contract in Hanafi fiqh. The central question is whether a mere offer and acceptance suffice to make the contract binding, or whether delivery of the pledged item is necessary. The passage presents the Hanafi majority view, an internal disagreement among Hanafi scholars, and the Maliki position for comparison. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between validity and bindingness in Islamic contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on constitution
Identify the base ruling: rahn is constituted (mun'aqid) by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). Note that some Hanafi scholars consider only ijab as the pillar (rukn), but the relied-upon view is that both are pillars.
The text explicitly says 'الأصح' (the most correct) that both are pillars.
2
Clarify the role of possession (qabd)
According to the Hanafi majority, possession is not a pillar of the contract but a condition for its bindingness (luzum). Therefore, without possession, the contract is valid (sahih) but not binding (ghayr lazim).
This contrasts with other contracts like sale, where possession is generally not required for bindingness.
3
Present the Maliki position
Imam Malik holds that rahn becomes binding immediately upon the contract (offer and acceptance) without requiring possession. His proofs are: (1) analogy to sale, as both sides involve wealth; (2) analogy to kafalah, as both are security contracts binding by acceptance alone.
The opposing school's argument is based on qiyas with commutative and security contracts.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school distinguishes rahn from sale because rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') from the debtor's side. Like gift and charity, it requires possession for bindingness. Thus, the analogy to kafalah is rejected because kafalah is a guarantee of a person, not a property-based security.
This response is implicit in the passage; the Hanafi response relies on the tabarru' nature of rahn from the rahin's perspective.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, if the pledge is not handed over, the contract is not binding; either party can cancel. Under Maliki law, the contract is binding immediately, and the creditor can compel delivery. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the majority view requiring qabd for luzum.
This difference matters in real transactions where possession may be delayed.

Source Text

امام مالک کا نقطہ نظر
وقال مالك يلزم بنفس العقد لانه يختص بالمال من الجانبين فصار كالبيع ولانه عقد وثيقة فاشبه الكفالة ترجمہ اور امام مالک نے فرمایا ہن لازم ہو جاتا ہے نفس عقد سے اس لئے کہ یہ مختص ہے جانہین سے مال کے ساتھ تو رہن مثل بیچ کے ہو گیا اور اس لئے کہ رہن مضبوطی کا ایک عقد ہے کہ یہ کفالت کے مشابہ ہو گیا۔

He said: Rahn is constituted by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) and is completed by possession (qabd). Some scholars said the pillar is only the offer, because it is a gratuitous contract, so it is completed by the benefactor like gift and charity. Possession is a condition for bindingness, as we will explain, Allah willing.
Translation: Quduri said that rahn is constituted by offer and acceptance and is completed by possession. Some mashayikh said that the pillar is only the offer because rahn is a gratuitous contract, so it is completed by the benefactor like gift and charity, and possession is a condition for bindingness according to the detail we will explain.
Explanation: Just like other contracts, rahn is constituted by offer and acceptance and completed by possession. This is the way of the majority of Hanafis. Shaykh al-Islam Khawahrizada said that only the offer is a pillar, and acceptance is not a pillar but a condition. However, the most correct view is that both are pillars.
Imam Khawahrizada said that rahn is a gratuitous contract, and a gratuitous contract is completed only by the benefactor, like gift and charity, which are completed by the benefactor alone. Possession is not a pillar but a condition for the bindingness of the rahn contract.
Imam Malik's viewpoint:
And Malik said: It becomes binding by the contract itself because it is specific to wealth from both sides, so it becomes like a sale, and because it is a contract of security, so it resembles kafalah.
Translation: Imam Malik said that rahn becomes binding by the very contract because it is specific to wealth from both sides, so rahn becomes like a sale, and because it is a contract of security, it resembles kafalah.
Explanation: Imam Malik's madhhab is that rahn becomes binding by mere offer and acceptance, and possession is not a condition. Rahn is like a sale; in a sale, both price and commodity are wealth, and possession is not a condition for bindingness. Similarly, in rahn, both debt and pledged property are wealth, and possession is not a condition for bindingness. Also, kafalah becomes binding by acceptance, without possession, so rahn should be similar since both are contracts of security.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses the constitution of the rahn contract. The base text (from Quduri) states that rahn is formed by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) and is completed by possession (qabd). The commentator explains that there is an internal Hanafi difference: some scholars, like Khawahrizada, consider only the offer as the rukn (pillar) because rahn is a tabarru' contract, and in tabarru' contracts the benefactor alone completes it (e.g., gift). However, the majority of Hanafis hold that both ijab and qabul are essential pillars. Then the passage presents Imam Malik's view, who holds that rahn becomes binding immediately upon the contract without needing possession. Malik argues by analogy to sale (both involve wealth from both sides) and to kafalah (a security contract that is binding by acceptance alone). The Hanafi position, however, requires possession for the contract to become binding (lazim). This difference has practical implications: under Hanafi law, if the rahn is not possessed, the creditor cannot enforce the pledge; under Maliki law, the contract is binding immediately.

Key Terms
ایجاب (ijab): The offer made by the rahin (debtor).
قبول (qabul): Acceptance by the murtahin (creditor).
رکن (rukn): Pillar or essential element without which the contract is invalid.
لزوم (luzum): Binding character of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the role of possession (qabd) in the rahn contract?
A) It is a pillar (rukn) without which the contract is invalid
B) It is a condition for the bindingness (luzum) of the contract
C) It is not required at all
D) It is recommended (sunnah)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a pledge contract (rahn) is formed by offer and acceptance but requires possession to become binding, unlike the Maliki school which deems it binding immediately.

8 min
Possession as Condition for Binding Rahn: Hanafi Position and Maliki Dispute
Explains Hanafi position on possession as condition for rahn, rebuts Maliki contrary view, notes intra-Hanafi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on possession (qabd) in rahn, explain the Maliki contrary view, and identify which view is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Rahn is binding only after possession; it is a gratuitous contract like wasiyyah, not like sale.

The contract of rahn (pledge) is a security device where property is given for a debt. In Hanafi fiqh, the contract is concluded by offer and acceptance but does not become binding until the pledgee takes possession. This lesson examines the evidence for this requirement, the contrary view of Imam Malik, and the intra-Hanafi difference on what constitutes valid possession. Understanding this will clarify when a pledge is enforceable and who bears loss before possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three layers of rahn contract
Recognize that rahn has three stages: conclusion (in'aqad) by offer and acceptance, completion (tamam) by possession, and bindingness (luzoom) also upon possession. The text from al-Quduri states: 'Rahn is concluded by offer and acceptance and completed by possession.' Note that possession is a condition for bindingness (shart al-luzoom), not for validity.
This distinguishes Hanafi from Maliki who considers it binding immediately.
2
Analyze the Hanafi evidence from the Quran and reason
The Hanafi proof is the verse 'farihanun maqbudah' (Quran 2:283). The description 'maqbudah' (possessed) generalizes to every rahn, and the verbal noun 'rihan' in the apodosis imports a command to take possession. Rationally, rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') because the pledgor receives nothing in return; thus it requires execution (nafadh) by possession, like wasiyyah.
The Quranic evidence is of strong authority (qat'i al-thubut) but the understanding (dalalah) is subject to ijtihad.
3
Contrast the Maliki position and its evidence
Imam Malik holds that rahn becomes binding by the contract alone without possession. His evidence: rahn involves property from both sides (debt and pledge) so it resembles a sale, which is binding without possession; also it is a security contract like kafalah, which binds by acceptance alone.
This difference leads to different consequences for loss before possession.
4
Note the Hanafi response to Malik
The Hanafi response: sale is commutative (mu'awadah) where both parties exchange property, so binding by contract; rahn is gratuitous (tabarru') so it requires execution. Kafalah is also gratuitous but binds by acceptance because it is a personal guarantee; rahn involves property and needs possession to establish the pledgee's lien.
The rational argument underscores the nature of rahn as a unilateral transfer of possession for security.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi difference on possession and the mufta bihi
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa and the majority hold that constructive possession (takhliyah) suffices, like in sale. Abu Yusuf requires actual physical transfer (naql) for movables because such possession initiates liability. The text states 'al-awwal asahh' (the first is more correct), meaning takhliyah is sufficient and this is the mufta bihi.
This nuance affects how a pledge is perfected for movable property.

تشریح ہم نے کہا تھا کہ قبضہ کے بغیر رہن تام نہیں ہوتا یہاں سے ہماری دلیل کا آغاز ہے۔ پہلی دلیل ... فرهان مقبوضة .... الخ یہاں رہن کو مقبوضہ ہونے کی صف سے متصف کیا گیا ہے۔ اور جب نکرہ کا وصف بیان کیا جاتا
ہے تو وہ عام ہوا کرتا ہے جیسے لا اكلم الا رجلا كوفیا تو اس سے یہ بات ثابت ہوئی کہ ہر رہن اس وصف کے ساتھ مشروع ہوگا۔ پھر رھان مصدر ہے جس پر فا داخل ہے اور رہان محل جزاء میں ہے اور جب ایسا ہو تو وہاں مصدر امر کے معنی میں ہوا کرتا ہے جیسے فضرب الرقاب میں اور فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة میں تو یہاں مصدر بمعنی امر ہے یعنی رہن کا معاملہ کرو اس حال میں کہ اس پر قبضہ کیا کرو قبضہ کے شرط ہونے پر دلیل عقلی: عقد تبرع ہے اور اس کے عقد تبرع ہونے پر دو دلیلیں ہیں۔
راہن پر جبر جائز نہیں کہ وہ رہن ہی رکھے۔
-۲ راہن کو رہن کے بدلہ میں مرتہن سے کوئی چیز حاصل نہیں ہوتی لہذا رہن کا امضاء و نفاذ ضروری ہے تا کہ مرتہن کا استحقاق ثابت ہو سکے جیسے وصیت بھی ایک تبرع ہے اور استحقاق موصی لہ کے واسطے نفاد ضروری ہے اور یہاں نفاذ قبضہ کے ذریعہ سے ہوگا بہر حال جواز رہن کے لئے قبضہ شرط ہے۔ پھر قبضہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں تحقیقی اور حکمی تحقیقی یہ ہے کہ قابض اس کو لے لے اور اپنے یہاں لیجائے اور حکمی سے مراد تخلیہ ہے یعنی راہن مرہون اور مرتہن کے درمیان تخلیہ کر دے جہاں سے مرتہن کو قبضہ کرنے میں کچھ دقت نہ ہو تو یہاں کونسا قبضہ شرط ہے تو مصنف نے فرمایا کہ ظاہر الروایہ میں قبضہ حکمی کافی ہے یعنی تخلیہ کافی ہے جیسے جب بائع نے مشتری اور مبیع کے درمیان تخلیہ کر دیا تو قبضہ کر گیا ایسے ہی یہاں بھی ہوگا اس لئے کہ قبضہ دونوں میں عقد مشروع کا حکم ہے تو دونوں میں تخلیہ کافی ہوگا۔

امام ابو یوسف کا نقطہ نظر
وعن ابي يوسف انه لا يثبت فى المنقول الا بالنقل لانه قبض موجب للضمان ابتداء بمنزلة الغصب بخلاف الشراء لانه ناقل للضمان من البائع الى المشترى وليس بموجب ابتداء والاول اصح
Book of Rahn. Ashraf al-Hidaya, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume 14. So from hadith it is known that rahn is permissible. Ijma' is established on the permissibility of rahn. Analogy: Kafalah (suretyship) is unanimously permissible, and its reality is that a right due from the surety can be collected, so due to kafalah, firmness in collection is achieved; similarly, due to rahn, firmness in collecting the right is achieved. So rahn is analogized to kafalah: just as kafalah is permissible, rahn is also permissible. Summary: from the four evidences, the validity of the contract of rahn is established. Rahn is concluded by offer and acceptance and completed by possession. Al-Quduri said: Rahn is concluded by offer and acceptance and completed by possession. Some scholars said: The pillar is solely the offer because it is a gratuitous contract, so it is completed by the donor, like gift and charity, and possession is a condition of binding, according to what we will explain if Allah wills. Commentary: Just as other contracts are concluded by offer and acceptance, so rahn is also concluded by offer and acceptance and completed by possession. This is the position of the majority of Hanafis. On this, Shaykh al-Islam Khawaharzadah says that the mere offer is the pillar and nothing else, and acceptance is not a pillar but a condition; however, the more correct view is that both are pillars. Imam Khawaharzadah said that rahn is a gratuitous contract, and a gratuitous contract is completed solely by the donor, like gift and charity, and possession is not a pillar but a condition for the bindingness of the contract of rahn. Imam Malik's viewpoint: Malik said: It becomes binding by the contract itself because it is specifically concerned with property from both sides, so it becomes like a sale, and because it is a contract of security, so it resembles kafalah. Commentary: Imam Malik's madhhab is that rahn becomes binding by mere offer and acceptance; possession is not a condition in it. Rahn is like a sale where both price and commodity are property; similarly, in rahn, both debt and pledged property are property. For a sale to be binding, possession is not a condition, so for rahn to be binding, possession will also not be a condition. Similarly, kafalah becomes binding by acceptance, and possession is not a condition in it. So likewise in rahn, possession will not be a condition for binding, given that both rahn and kafalah are contracts of security. Hanafi's evidence: Our evidence is the verse we have recited, and the verbal noun connected to the letter fa' in the position of the apodosis carries the meaning of command. And because rahn is a gratuitous contract in that the pledgor does not acquire anything in return from the pledgee, therefore he is not compelled [to pledge], so it must be executed like a wasiyyah, and that execution is by possession. Then in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), takhliyah (vacating) is sufficient for possession because it is possession by virtue of a legitimate contract, so it resembles possession of a sold item. Commentary: We said that without possession rahn is not complete. Here begins our evidence. First evidence: 'farihanun maqbudah'... here rahn is described with the attribute of being possessed. When an indefinite noun is described, it becomes general, like 'I will not speak except to a Kufi man.' Thus it is established that every rahn is prescribed with this attribute. Then 'rihan' is a verbal noun upon which 'fa' enters, and 'rihan' is in the position of apodosis. When that happens, the verbal noun carries the meaning of command, like 'fadarbu al-riqab' and 'fatahriru raqabatin mu'minah'. So here the verbal noun is in the meaning of command: 'Deal in rahn in such a way that you take possession of it.' Rational evidence: it is a gratuitous contract, and there are two proofs for it being gratuitous: 1) The pledgor cannot be compelled to pledge. 2) The pledgor does not receive anything in return from the pledgee. Therefore, the rahn must be executed and made effective so that the pledgee's entitlement is established, like a wasiyyah which is also a gratuitous transfer and its effectiveness requires execution, which here is by possession. In any case, possession is a condition for the validity of rahn. Then possession is of two types: actual (tahqiqi) and constructive (hukmi). Actual is that the possessor takes it and brings it to his place. Constructive means takhliyah, i.e., the pledgor vacates between the pledged item and the pledgee so that the pledgee has no difficulty in taking possession. Which possession is the condition? The author said that in the apparent narration, constructive possession is sufficient, i.e., takhliyah is sufficient, just as when a seller vacates between the buyer and the sold item, possession is considered taken. So here too, because possession in both is by virtue of a legitimate contract, takhliyah is sufficient in both. Imam Abu Yusuf's viewpoint: From Abu Yusuf: 'It is not established in movable property except by transfer, because it is possession that initiates liability like usurpation, unlike purchase because it transfers liability from seller to buyer and does not initiate it.' And the first is more correct.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi position on the contract of rahn (pledge), particularly the requirement of possession for its completion and bindingness. The author begins by establishing the permissibility of rahn through the four sources: hadith, ijma, qiyas (analogy to kafalah). Then the text from al-Quduri's matn is cited: 'Rahn is concluded by offer and acceptance and completed by possession.' This indicates that in Hanafi fiqh, rahn is a valid contract even without possession, but it only becomes fully binding (lazim) upon possession. The commentator explains that the majority of Hanafis hold that both offer and acceptance are pillars of the contract, though some scholars (like Khawaharzadah) considered only the offer as the pillar, arguing that rahn is a gratuitous contract completed by the offeror alone. However, the correct view is that both are pillars. Imam Malik's contrary view is then presented: he holds that rahn becomes binding immediately upon offer and acceptance, without requiring possession. His evidence is that rahn involves property from both sides like a sale, and it is a security contract like kafalah, both of which do not require possession for bindingness. The Hanafis respond with evidence from the Quran: the verse 'farihanun maqbudah' (Quran 2:283) uses a descriptive clause that generalizes the requirement of possession for every rahn. Moreover, the word 'rihan' (pledges) in the apodosis of a conditional clause gives it an imperative meaning, so the verse effectively commands taking possession. Rationally, rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') like wasiyyah, which requires execution (tamkin) through possession to confer entitlement; without possession, the contract is not binding. The passage then clarifies the type of possession required: in the 'zahir al-riwayah' (the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani), takhliyah (vacating the property for the pledgee) is sufficient as constructive possession, analogous to sale where vacating suffices. Finally, an intra-Hanafi difference is noted: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that for movable property (manqul), actual physical transfer is required, not just takhliyah, because such possession initiates liability (daman) like usurpation, whereas in sale it merely transfers liability. However, the first view (takhliyah sufficing) is deemed more correct. This passage provides a thorough legal analysis of the condition of possession in rahn, covering the main Hanafi position, the Maliki opposition, and a nuance within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract of pledge where property is given as security for a debt; concluded by offer and acceptance, completed by possession.
قبض (qabd): Possession; constructive possession (takhliyah) suffices in zahir al-riwayah, but Abu Yusuf requires actual transfer for movables.
عقد تبرع (aqd tabarru'): A gratuitous contract where one party receives no material benefit; includes gift, charity, and rahn, requiring execution by possession.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani; the relied-upon transmission in the Hanafi school.
تخلیہ (takhliyah): Vacating the property; making it accessible to the pledgee without physical transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a rahn (pledge) become binding (lazim)?
A) Immediately upon offer and acceptance
B) Upon possession (qabd) by the pledgee
C) Upon payment of the debt
D) Upon registration of the contract

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, rahn requires possession to become binding because it is a gratuitous contract; the Quranic description 'farihanun maqbudah' supports this requirement.

Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
7 min
Possession in Rahn: Hanafi Proof and Abu Yusuf's View
Proof and intra-madhhab dispute on possession in pawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof that possession (qabd) is a condition for bindingness in rahn and compare Imam Abu Yusuf's view that actual transfer is required for movables.

Core Idea
Rahn requires possession to become binding; constructive possession suffices per Zahir al-Riwayah.

This passage presents the Hanafi school's proof from the Quran and rational analogy that possession (qabd) is necessary for a pawn (rahn) contract to become binding. It then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that for movable property only actual physical transfer suffices, while the majority view (Zahir al-Riwayah) accepts constructive possession (takhliya). The author declares the majority view more correct, shaping the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary proof from Quran
Locate the Quranic verse 'farihanun maqbudatun' (2:283) and understand how the qualifying phrase 'maqbudatun' indicates that rahn is prescribed with possession.
The indefinite noun with qualification creates generality, requiring every rahn to be possessed.
2
Analyze the grammatical interpretation
Note that the verbal noun 'rihan' with the particle 'fa' in the result clause is interpreted as a command (amr) to conduct rahn with possession, similar to 'fadribu al-riqab' in another verse.
3
Understand the rational proof (tabarru' analogy)
Recognize that rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') because the pawner receives nothing in return; therefore, it must be made binding through possession, analogous to a will (wasiyya).
This establishes that possession is a condition (shart) for bindingness (luzum), not for validity (sihha).
4
Differentiate the two types of possession
Distinguish actual possession (tahqiqi: physical transfer) from constructive possession (hukmi: takhliya). The Zahir al-Riwayah holds takhliya sufficient, analogizing to sale.
Constructive possession means the pawner vacates the property for the pawnee without obstruction.
5
Analyze Abu Yusuf's objection and the tarjih
Imam Abu Yusuf argues that possession in rahn imposes liability (daman) ab initio like usurpation, requiring actual transfer for movables. The author prefers the majority view (takhliya suffices) as more correct (asahh).
The practical difference: under the majority, if the pawner vacates but the pawnee doesn't physically take, possession is established; under Abu Yusuf, it is not.
